<th scope="row">Battles/wars</th>
<td><a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a><br />
&#160;• <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Trenton" title="Battle of Trenton">Battle of Trenton</a></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>James Monroe</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/?/ 'a' in 'about'">?</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span><span title="/?/ primary stress follows">?</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/o?/ long 'o' in 'code'">o?</span></span>/</a></span></span>; April 28, 1758 – July 4, 1831) was the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">fifth</a> <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a> (1817–1825). Monroe was the last president who was a <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">Founding Father of the United States</a> and the last president from the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_dynasty" title="Virginia dynasty">Virginian dynasty</a> and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States_by_date_of_birth#Generation" title="List of Presidents of the United States by date of birth">Republican Generation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> Born in <a href="/wiki/Westmoreland_County,_Virginia" title="Westmoreland County, Virginia">Westmoreland County</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>, Monroe was of the planter class and fought in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>. He was wounded in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Trenton" title="Battle of Trenton">Battle of Trenton</a> with a musket ball to his shoulder. After studying law under <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> from 1780 to 1783, he served as a <a href="/wiki/List_of_delegates_to_the_Continental_Congress" title="List of delegates to the Continental Congress">delegate</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Congress" title="Continental Congress">Continental Congress</a>. As an <a href="/wiki/Anti-Federalism" title="Anti-Federalism">anti-federalist</a> delegate to the Virginia convention that considered ratification of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a>, Monroe opposed <a href="/wiki/Ratification" title="Ratification">ratification</a>, claiming it gave too much power to the central government. He took an active part in the new government, and in 1790 he was elected to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">Senate</a> of <a href="/wiki/1st_United_States_Congress" title="1st United States Congress">the first United States Congress</a>, where he joined the <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Jeffersonians</a>. He gained experience as an executive as the <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Virginia" title="Governor of Virginia">Governor of Virginia</a> and rose to national prominence as a diplomat in France, when he helped negotiate the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</a> in 1803. During the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>, Monroe held the critical roles of <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_War" title="United States Secretary of War">Secretary of War</a> under President <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hart1_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hart1-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Facing little opposition from the fractured <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist Party</a>, Monroe was <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1816" title="United States presidential election, 1816">easily elected</a> president in 1816, winning over 80 percent of the electoral vote and becoming the last president during the <a href="/wiki/First_Party_System" title="First Party System">First Party System</a> era of American politics. As president, he <a href="/wiki/Adams%E2%80%93On%C3%ADs_Treaty" title="Adams–Onís Treaty">bought Florida from Spain</a> and sought to ease partisan tensions, embarking on a tour of the country that was generally well received. With the ratification of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_1818" title="Treaty of 1818">Treaty of 1818</a>, under the successful diplomacy of his Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a>, the United States extended from the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a>, giving America harbor and fishing rights in the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Northwest" title="Pacific Northwest">Pacific Northwest</a>. The United States and Britain jointly occupied the <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Country" title="Oregon Country">Oregon Country</a>. In addition to the acquisition of Florida, the landmark <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_1819" title="Treaty of 1819" class="mw-redirect">Treaty of 1819</a> secured the border of the United States along the <a href="/wiki/42nd_parallel_north" title="42nd parallel north">42nd Parallel</a> to the Pacific Ocean and represented America's first determined attempt at creating an "American global empire".<sup id="cite_ref-Weeks_p1_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks_p1-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> As nationalism surged, partisan fury subsided and the "<a href="/wiki/Era_of_Good_Feelings" title="Era of Good Feelings">Era of Good Feelings</a>" ensued until the <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1819" title="Panic of 1819">Panic of 1819</a> struck and <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Compromise" title="Missouri Compromise">dispute over the admission of Missouri</a> embroiled the country in 1820. Nonetheless, Monroe <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1820" title="United States presidential election, 1820">won near-unanimous reelection</a>.</p>
<p>Monroe supported the founding of <a href="/wiki/American_Colonization_Society" title="American Colonization Society">colonies in Africa for free African Americans</a> that would eventually form the nation of <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>, whose capital, <a href="/wiki/Monrovia" title="Monrovia">Monrovia</a>, is named in his honor. In 1823, he announced the United States' opposition to any European intervention in the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_American_wars_of_independence" title="Spanish American wars of independence">recently independent countries</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</a>, which became a landmark in American foreign policy. His presidency concluded the first period of American presidential history before the beginning of <a href="/wiki/Jacksonian_democracy" title="Jacksonian democracy">Jacksonian democracy</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Second_Party_System" title="Second Party System">Second Party System</a> era. Following his retirement in 1825, Monroe was plagued by financial difficulties. He died in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> on July 4, 1831.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JamesMonroeHomesiteMarker.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/JamesMonroeHomesiteMarker.jpg/170px-JamesMonroeHomesiteMarker.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/JamesMonroeHomesiteMarker.jpg/255px-JamesMonroeHomesiteMarker.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/JamesMonroeHomesiteMarker.jpg/340px-JamesMonroeHomesiteMarker.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="2048" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JamesMonroeHomesiteMarker.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Marker designating the site of James Monroe's birthplace in <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Hall,_Virginia" title="Monroe Hall, Virginia">Monroe Hall, Virginia</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>James Monroe was born on April 28, 1758, in his parents' house located in a wooded area of Westmoreland County, <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>. The site is marked and is one mile from the unincorporated community known today as <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Hall,_Virginia" title="Monroe Hall, Virginia">Monroe Hall, Virginia</a>. The <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe_Family_Home_Site" title="James Monroe Family Home Site">James Monroe Family Home Site</a> was listed on the <a href="/wiki/National_Register_of_Historic_Places" title="National Register of Historic Places">National Register of Historic Places</a> in 1979.<sup id="cite_ref-nris_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nris-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> His father Spence Monroe (1727–1774) was a moderately prosperous planter who also practiced carpentry. His mother Elizabeth Jones (1730–1774) married Spence Monroe in 1752 and they had several children.<sup id="cite_ref-Harry_Ammon_1990_p._577_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harry_Ammon_1990_p._577-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>His paternal great-grandfather Patrick Andrew Monroe emigrated to America from Scotland in the mid-17th century. In 1650 he patented a large tract of land in Washington Parish, <a href="/wiki/Westmoreland_County,_Virginia" title="Westmoreland County, Virginia">Westmoreland County, Virginia</a>. Among James Monroe's ancestors were <a href="/wiki/French_Huguenot" title="French Huguenot" class="mw-redirect">French Huguenot</a> immigrants, who came to Virginia in 1700.<sup id="cite_ref-Harry_Ammon_1990_p._577_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harry_Ammon_1990_p._577-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>First tutored at home by his mother Elizabeth, between the ages of 11 and 16, the young Monroe studied at Campbell town Academy, a school run by Reverend Archibald Campbell of Washington Parish. There he excelled as a pupil and progressed through Latin and mathematics faster than most boys his age. <a href="/wiki/John_Marshall" title="John Marshall">John Marshall</a>, later <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice of the United States</a>, was among his classmates.</p>
<p>Upon the death of his father in 1774, Monroe inherited his small <a href="/wiki/Plantation" title="Plantation">plantation</a> and slaves, officially joining the ruling class of the planter elite in what had become the slave society of Virginia.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Sixteen years old, he began forming a close relationship with his maternal uncle, the influential <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Jones_(Virginia)" title="Joseph Jones (Virginia)">Judge Joseph Jones</a>, who had been educated at the <a href="/wiki/Inns_of_Court" title="Inns of Court">Inns of Court</a> in London and was the executor of his father's estate. That same year, Monroe enrolled in the <a href="/wiki/College_of_William_and_Mary" title="College of William and Mary" class="mw-redirect">College of William and Mary</a>. At the time, most students were charged with excitement over the prospect of rebellion against <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">King George III</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_service_in_the_Revolutionary_War_and_serious_wounding">Military service in the Revolutionary War and serious wounding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Military service in the Revolutionary War and serious wounding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Trenton_by_Charles_McBarron.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Battle_of_Trenton_by_Charles_McBarron.jpg/220px-Battle_of_Trenton_by_Charles_McBarron.jpg" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Battle_of_Trenton_by_Charles_McBarron.jpg/330px-Battle_of_Trenton_by_Charles_McBarron.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Battle_of_Trenton_by_Charles_McBarron.jpg/440px-Battle_of_Trenton_by_Charles_McBarron.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1931" data-file-height="1407" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Trenton_by_Charles_McBarron.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Trenton" title="Battle of Trenton">Battle of Trenton</a> and Hessian guns seized by Virginians led by Captain <a href="/wiki/William_Washington" title="William Washington">William Washington</a> and Lt. Monroe who were severely wounded</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In early 1776, about a year and a half after his enrollment, Monroe dropped out of college and joined the 3rd Virginia Regiment (established December 28, 1775) in the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Army" title="Continental Army">Continental Army</a> where his background as a college student and the son of a well-known planter enabled him to obtain an officer's commission. He never returned to earn a degree.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> In June 1775, after the battles of Lexington and Concord, Monroe, and some other William and Mary students joined 24 older men in raiding the arsenal at the <a href="/wiki/Governor%27s_Palace_(Williamsburg,_Virginia)" title="Governor's Palace (Williamsburg, Virginia)">Governor's Palace</a> in Williamsburg. They used the loot of 200 muskets and 300 swords to arm the Williamsburg <a href="/wiki/Militia" title="Militia">militia</a>.</p>
<p>Although <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a> served as a courier in a militia unit at age thirteen, Monroe is regarded as the last U.S. President who was a <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">Revolutionary War</a> veteran, since he served as an officer of the Continental Army and took part in combat.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> With the rest of Washington's army, Monroe's regiment was chased off Long Island in the fall of 1776 and down the length of New Jersey crossing the Delaware River in December 1776. Down to mere days before their enlistments expired, the Army's commander, General <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">Washington</a> decided that only a bold step could save the Army and the revolutionary cause from oblivion. Washington ordered his force, that had shrunk 90% since the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Long_Island" title="Battle of Long Island">Battle of Brooklyn</a> which is also known by the wider name, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Long_Island" title="Battle of Long Island">Battle of Long Island</a>, and down to under 3,000 effective soldiers, to cross the Delaware River on Christmas Day 1776 in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Trenton" title="Battle of Trenton">Battle of Trenton</a>. Monroe and his regiment crossed over and marched through a <a href="/wiki/Nor%27easter" title="Nor'easter">Nor'easter</a> snow storm north and then east towards Trenton. Along the way, the soldiers were spotted by a young patriot doctor, John Riker, whose dogs had been awakened in the pre-dawn early morning. Riker volunteered to lend his medical bag to the efforts saying that as many doctors as possible would be needed fearing severe casualties from a clash with the battle-tested German-speaking Hessian professional mercenary soldiers. Avoiding detection, the Americans approached the center of Trenton from north and south. When the Hessians sounded the alarm, they tried to get several of their artillery pieces in action to pour grapeshot into the Americans marching down towards the homes they had commandeered. Knowing that this would slow the assault, after a volley of artillery fire, Lieutenant Monroe and General Washington's cousin, Captain William Washington and their men rushed to seize the guns before they could fire. Both young officers were severely wounded. Captain Washington was badly wounded in both hands, and young Lieutenant James Monroe was carried from the field bleeding badly after he was struck in the left shoulder by a musket ball, which severed an artery. It would be the young volunteer doctor, John Riker who clamped the artery, keeping him from bleeding to death and saving the life of a man who would go on to achieve so much in politics both as a Virginian and on the national stage as a future President.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> The wounded soldier, Monroe was sent home to Virginia to nurse his injuries. The Battle of Trenton would be Monroe's only battle as he would spend the next three months recuperating from his wound. In <a href="/wiki/John_Trumbull" title="John Trumbull">John Trumbull</a>'s painting <i>Capture of the <a href="/wiki/Hessian_(soldiers)" title="Hessian (soldiers)" class="mw-redirect">Hessians</a> at the Battle of Trenton</i>, Monroe can be seen lying wounded at left center of the painting. In the famous painting, <i><a href="/wiki/Washington_Crossing_the_Delaware" title="Washington Crossing the Delaware">Washington Crossing the Delaware</a></i>, Monroe is depicted holding the American flag.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After recuperating from his wound, he was appointed as a lieutenant colonel in the Virginia militia, and tasked to recruit and lead a regiment, but the regiment was never raised. He returned to Williamsburg in September of 1779 and studied law with <a href="/wiki/George_Wythe" title="George Wythe">George Wythe</a>, and then moved to Richmond to study law with Thomas Jefferson. In 1780 the British invaded Richmond, and as <a href="/wiki/List_of_Governors_of_Virginia" title="List of Governors of Virginia">Governor</a>, Jefferson commissioned Monroe as a colonel to command the militia raised in response and act as liaison to the Continental Army in North Carolina.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Monroe resumed studying law under <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Jefferson</a>, and continued until 1783.<sup id="cite_ref-isbn0-19-530092-0_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-19-530092-0-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-log-cabin-76_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-log-cabin-76-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> Monroe was not particularly interested in legal theory or practice, but chose to take it up because he thought that it offered "the most immediate rewards" and could ease his path to wealth, social standing, and political influence.<sup id="cite_ref-log-cabin-76_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-log-cabin-76-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> After passing the bar, he practiced law in <a href="/wiki/Fredericksburg,_Virginia" title="Fredericksburg, Virginia">Fredericksburg, Virginia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Library_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Library-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage_and_family">Marriage and family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Marriage and family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>James Monroe married <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Kortright_Monroe" title="Elizabeth Kortright Monroe" class="mw-redirect">Elizabeth Kortright</a> (1768–1830),<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> daughter of Laurence Kortright and Hannah Aspinwall Kortright, on February 16, 1786, in New York City. He had met her while serving with the Continental Congress, which then met in New York, the temporary capital of the new nation. After a brief honeymoon on <a href="/wiki/Long_Island,_New_York" title="Long Island, New York" class="mw-redirect">Long Island, New York</a>, the Monroes returned to New York City to live with her father until Congress adjourned. The Monroes had the following children:</p>
<ul>
<li>Eliza Kortright Monroe Hay (1786–1840): Eliza was born in Fredericksburg, Virginia, in 1786, and was educated in Paris at the school of <a href="/wiki/Madame_Campan" title="Madame Campan" class="mw-redirect">Madame Campan</a> during the time her father was the United States Ambassador to France. In 1808 she married <a href="/wiki/George_Hay_(Virginia)" title="George Hay (Virginia)">George Hay</a>, a prominent Virginia attorney who had served as prosecutor in the trial of <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Burr" title="Aaron Burr">Aaron Burr</a> and later U.S. District Judge. Their daughter, Hortense, was named in honor of her childhood friend, <a href="/wiki/Hortense_de_Beauharnais" title="Hortense de Beauharnais">Hortense de Beauharnais</a>, step-daughter of Napoleon. Eliza alienated most of Washington society for her refusal to call on wives of the diplomatic corps, as was the custom, and caused another social furor in closing her sister's wedding to all but family and friends. For all her apparent vanity, however, she demonstrated genuine compassion during the fever epidemic that swept Washington during her father's Presidency. She spent many sleepless nights selflessly caring for victims. Following the deaths of her husband and father, Eliza moved to Paris, France, where she died on January 27, 1840.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>James Spence Monroe (1799–1801) – his grave reads "J.S. Monroe", so the proper names are speculative but typical of naming patterns of the time, which passed on family names.</li>
<li>Maria Hester Monroe (1804–1850) – married her cousin <a href="/wiki/Samuel_L._Gouverneur" title="Samuel L. Gouverneur">Samuel L. Gouverneur</a> on March 8, 1820, in the first wedding of a president's child in the White House.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Plantations_and_slavery">Plantations and slavery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Plantations and slavery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:OakHillfront.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/OakHillfront.jpg/220px-OakHillfront.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/92/OakHillfront.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/92/OakHillfront.jpg 2x" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="168" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:OakHillfront.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<center><b>Oak Hill Mansion</b></center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Monroe sold his small inherited Virginia plantation in 1783 to enter law and politics. Monroe later fulfilled his youthful dream of becoming the owner of a large <a href="/wiki/Plantation" title="Plantation">plantation</a> and wielding great political power, but his plantation was never profitable. Although he owned much more land and slaves and speculated in property, he was rarely on-site to oversee the operations. Overseers treated the slaves harshly to force production, but the plantations barely broke even. Monroe incurred debts by his lavish and expensive lifestyle and often sold property (including slaves) to pay them off.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Monroe was a wealthy <a href="/wiki/Loudoun_County,_Virginia" title="Loudoun County, Virginia">Loudoun County</a> plantation owner who owned numerous slave plantations including <a href="/wiki/Oak_Hill_(James_Monroe_House)" title="Oak Hill (James Monroe House)">Oak Hill</a> in <a href="/wiki/Aldie,_Virginia" title="Aldie, Virginia">Aldie</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Stevenson_p159_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stevenson_p159-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> Monroe was an absentee slaveholder in that he had his overseers run the plantations while he lived elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-Stevenson_p159_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stevenson_p159-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> Overseers moved or separated slave families from different Monroe plantations in accordance with production and maintenance needs of each satellite plantation.<sup id="cite_ref-Stevenson_p159_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stevenson_p159-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> One of Monroe's slaves named Daniel often ran away from Monroe's plantation in <a href="/wiki/Albemarle_County,_Virginia" title="Albemarle County, Virginia">Albermarle County</a>, to visit other slaves or separated family members.<sup id="cite_ref-Stevenson_p160_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stevenson_p160-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> Monroe commonly referred to Daniel as a "scoundrel" and described the "worthlessness" of Daniel as a runaway slave.<sup id="cite_ref-Stevenson_p160_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stevenson_p160-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> Monroe's practice of moving and separating slave families was common treatment of slaves in the South.<sup id="cite_ref-Stevenson_p160_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stevenson_p160-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_political_career">Early political career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Early political career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Virginia_politics">Virginia politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Virginia politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Monroe was elected to the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_House_of_Delegates" title="Virginia House of Delegates">Virginia House of Delegates</a> in 1782. He was elected to Congress in November 1783 and served in Annapolis until Congress left for Trenton in June 1784. He had served a total of three years when he finally retired from that office by the rule of rotation.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> By that time, the government was meeting in the temporary capital of <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>.</p>
<p>In Virginia, the struggle in 1788 over the ratification of the proposed Constitution involved more than a simple clash between federalists and anti-federalists. Virginians held a full spectrum of opinions about the merits of the proposed change in national government. <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a> were leading supporters; <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Henry" title="Patrick Henry">Patrick Henry</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_Mason" title="George Mason">George Mason</a> were leading opponents. Those who held the middle ground in the ideological struggle became the central figures. Led by Monroe and <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Pendleton" title="Edmund Pendleton">Edmund Pendleton</a>, these "federalists who are for amendments," criticized the absence of a <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_rights" title="Bill of rights">bill of rights</a> and worried about surrendering taxation powers to the central government. Virginia ratified the Constitution in June 1788, largely because Monroe, Pendleton and followers suspended their reservations and vowed to press for changes after the new government had been established.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Virginia narrowly ratified the Constitution. Monroe ran for a House seat in the <a href="/wiki/1st_United_States_Congress" title="1st United States Congress">First Congress</a> but was defeated by Madison. In 1790 he was elected by the Virginia legislature as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">United States Senator</a>. He soon joined the <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">"Democratic-Republican" faction</a> led by Jefferson and Madison, and by 1791 was the party leader in the Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ambassador_to_France">Ambassador to France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Ambassador to France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Monroe_(1758-1831).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/James_Monroe_%281758-1831%29.jpg/220px-James_Monroe_%281758-1831%29.jpg" width="220" height="245" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/James_Monroe_%281758-1831%29.jpg/330px-James_Monroe_%281758-1831%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/James_Monroe_%281758-1831%29.jpg/440px-James_Monroe_%281758-1831%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1076" data-file-height="1200" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Monroe_(1758-1831).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The earliest preserved portrait of James Monroe as Minister Plenipotentiary to France in 1794.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Monroe resigned his Senate seat after being appointed <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_France" title="United States Ambassador to France">Minister to France</a> in 1794.<sup id="cite_ref-bio.congress_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bio.congress-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> As ambassador, Monroe secured the release of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Paine" title="Thomas Paine">Thomas Paine</a> in revolutionary France after his arrest for opposition to the execution of <a href="/wiki/Louis_XVI" title="Louis XVI" class="mw-redirect">Louis XVI</a>. The government insisted that Paine be deported to the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Monroe arranged to free all the Americans held in French prisons. He also gained the freedom of <a href="/wiki/Adrienne_de_La_Fayette" title="Adrienne de La Fayette">Adrienne de La Fayette</a> and issued her and her family American passports (they had been granted <a href="/wiki/Honorary_Citizen_of_the_United_States" title="Honorary Citizen of the United States" class="mw-redirect">citizenship</a> by the U.S. government for contributions during the Revolution.) She used that for travel to her <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_du_Motier,_marquis_de_Lafayette" title="Gilbert du Motier, marquis de Lafayette" class="mw-redirect">husband</a>, imprisoned in <a href="/wiki/Olmutz" title="Olmutz" class="mw-redirect">Olmutz</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A strong friend of the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>, Monroe tried to assure France that <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>'s policy of strict neutrality did not favor Britain. But American policy had come to favor Britain, and Monroe was stunned by the United States' signing of the <a href="/wiki/Jay_Treaty" title="Jay Treaty">Jay Treaty</a> in London. With France and Britain at war, the Jay Treaty alarmed and angered the French. Washington had differences with Monroe and discharged him as Minister to France, claiming his "inefficiency, disruptive maneuvers, and failure to safeguard the interests of his country."<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Monroe had long been concerned about foreign influence on the presidency. He was alarmed by the Spanish diplomat <a href="/wiki/Don_Diego_de_Gardoqui" title="Don Diego de Gardoqui">Don Diego de Gardoqui</a>, who in 1785 tried to convince Congress to allow Spain to close the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a> to American traffic for 30 years. Spain controlled much of the Mississippi since taking over former French territory, including the important port of New Orleans. Monroe thought that Spain could have endangered the U.S. retention of its Southwest and caused the dominance of the Northeast.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> Monroe believed in both a strong presidency and the system of checks and balances.</p>
<p>In the 1790s he fretted over an aging George Washington being too much influenced by close advisers such as <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a>, whom Monroe thought too close to Britain. He was humiliated by Washington's criticism for his support of revolutionary France as minister to the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Governor_of_Virginia_and_diplomat">Governor of Virginia and diplomat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Governor of Virginia and diplomat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Out of office, Monroe returned to practicing law in <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a> until elected <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Virginia" title="Governor of Virginia">governor</a> there as a Democratic-Republican, his first term serving from 1799 to 1802. He was reelected Virginia's governor in 1811.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> He called out the state militia to suppress <a href="/wiki/Gabriel%27s_Rebellion" title="Gabriel's Rebellion" class="mw-redirect">Gabriel's Rebellion</a>. Gabriel and 26 other enslaved people who participated were all hanged for treason. Monroe thought that foreign and Federalist elements had created the <a href="/wiki/Quasi_War" title="Quasi War" class="mw-redirect">Quasi War</a> of 1798–1800 and were behind efforts to prevent the election of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> as president in 1800. As governor he considered using the Virginia militia to force the outcome in favor of Jefferson.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> Federalists were likewise suspicious of Monroe, some seeing him as at best a French dupe and at worst a traitor.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>President Jefferson sent Monroe to France to assist <a href="/wiki/Robert_Livingston_(1746%E2%80%931813)" title="Robert Livingston (1746–1813)" class="mw-redirect">Robert R. Livingston</a> in negotiating the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</a>. Monroe was then appointed <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_Great_Britain" title="United States Ambassador to Great Britain" class="mw-redirect">Minister to the Court of St. James's</a> in London from 1803 to 1807. In 1806 he negotiated a treaty with Great Britain, known as the <a href="/wiki/Monroe%E2%80%93Pinkney_Treaty" title="Monroe–Pinkney Treaty">Monroe–Pinkney Treaty</a>. It would have extended the <a href="/wiki/Jay_Treaty" title="Jay Treaty">Jay Treaty</a> of 1794 which had expired after ten years. Jefferson had fought the Jay Treaty intensely in 1794–95 because he felt it would allow the British to subvert <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">American republicanism</a>. The treaty had produced ten years of peace and highly lucrative trade for American merchants, but Jefferson was still hostile. When Monroe and the British signed a renewal in December 1806, Jefferson decided not to even submit it to the Senate for ratification. Although the new treaty called for ten more years of trade between the United States and the British Empire and gave American merchants guarantees that would have been good for business, Jefferson refused to give up the potential weapon of commercial warfare against Britain and was unhappy that it did not end the hated British practice of <a href="/wiki/Impressment" title="Impressment">impressment</a> of American sailors. Jefferson did not attempt to obtain another treaty, and as a result, the two nations drifted from peace toward the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> After the failure of the treaty, Monroe returned to the United States in 1807. Monroe was deeply hurt by the administration's repudiation of the treaty, and Monroe fell out with Secretary of State James Madison.<sup id="cite_ref-leibiger_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leibiger-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1808_election_and_the_Quids">1808 election and the Quids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 1808 election and the Quids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Democratic-Republican Party was increasingly factionalized, with "<a href="/wiki/Old_Republicans" title="Old Republicans" class="mw-redirect">Old Republicans</a>" or "Quids" denouncing the Jefferson administration for abandoning true republican principles. The Quids, seeing that Monroe's foreign policy had been rejected by Jefferson, tried to enlist Monroe in their cause. The plan was to run Monroe for president in the 1808 election in cooperation with the <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist Party</a>, which had a strong base in New England. <a href="/wiki/John_Randolph_of_Roanoke" title="John Randolph of Roanoke">John Randolph of Roanoke</a> led the Quid effort to stop Jefferson's choice of <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a>. However, the regular Democratic-Republicans overcame the Quids in the nominating caucus, kept control of the party in Virginia, and protected Madison's base. Monroe was not a candidate for president, and Madison was elected.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> After the election Monroe quickly reconciled with Jefferson, but did not speak with Madison, a former friend, until 1810.<sup id="cite_ref-leibiger_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leibiger-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Secretary_of_State_and_Secretary_of_War">Secretary of State and Secretary of War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Secretary of State and Secretary of War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Monroe returned to the Virginia House of Burgesses and was elected to another term as governor in 1811, but only served four months. In April 1811, Madison appointed Monroe as Secretary of State in hopes of shoring up the support of the more radical factions of the Democratic-Republicans.<sup id="cite_ref-leibiger_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leibiger-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> Monroe had little to do with the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>, as President Madison and the War Hawks in Congress were dominant. The war went very badly, and when the British burned the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Capitol" title="U.S. Capitol" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Capitol</a> and the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> on August 24, 1814, Madison removed John Armstrong as Secretary of War and turned to Monroe for help, appointing him <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_War" title="United States Secretary of War">Secretary of War</a> on September 27.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> Monroe resigned as Secretary of State on October 1, but no successor was ever appointed and thus from October 1, 1814 to February 28, 1815, Monroe effectively held both Cabinet posts. Monroe formulated plans for an offensive invasion of <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> to win the war, but a peace treaty was ratified in February 1815, before any armies moved north. Monroe therefore resigned as Secretary of War on March 15, 1815 and was formally reappointed Secretary of State. Monroe stayed on at State until March 4, 1817, when he began his term as the new President of the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Hart1_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hart1-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidential_elections_of_1816_and_1820">Presidential elections of 1816 and 1820</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Presidential elections of 1816 and 1820">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1816" title="United States presidential election, 1816">United States presidential election, 1816</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1820" title="United States presidential election, 1820">United States presidential election, 1820</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_United_States" title="Congress of the United States" class="mw-redirect">Congressional</a> nominating caucus experienced little opposition during the administrations of Jefferson and Madison, but this situation changed in the election year of 1816. An indeterminate number of anti-Virginia Republicans, led by the New York delegation, objected to the caucus system along with the Federalists. Disorganization and failure to agree on <a href="/wiki/William_H._Crawford" title="William H. Crawford">William H. Crawford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Tompkins" title="Daniel Tompkins" class="mw-redirect">Daniel Tompkins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a> or another possible contender weakened opposition to Monroe. The boycott by Virginia delegates of the March 12 caucus removed the chances of Monroe's opponents, and he received the caucus nomination four days later.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> With the <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist Party</a> in disarray due to the unpopularity of their opposition to the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a>, Monroe easily won election.<sup id="cite_ref-miller.monroe.elections_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-miller.monroe.elections-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> The Federalists did not even name a candidate, though <a href="/wiki/Rufus_King" title="Rufus King">Rufus King</a> of New York did run in opposition to Monroe under the Federalist banner.<sup id="cite_ref-miller.monroe.elections_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-miller.monroe.elections-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> King carried only Connecticut, Delaware, and Massachusetts and won only 34 of 217 electoral votes cast.<sup id="cite_ref-miller.monroe.elections_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-miller.monroe.elections-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The collapse of the Federalists left Monroe with no organized opposition at the end of his first term, and he ran for reelection unopposed,<sup id="cite_ref-miller.monroe.elections_40-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-miller.monroe.elections-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> the only president other than <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">Washington</a> to do so. A single elector from New Hampshire cast a vote for <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a>, preventing a unanimous vote in the <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College" title="Electoral College" class="mw-redirect">Electoral College</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-miller.monroe.elections_40-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-miller.monroe.elections-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency">Presidency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Presidency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width: 230px;">
<div style="width: 230px; height: 270px; overflow: hidden;">
<div style="position: relative; top: -47px; left: -35px; width: 300px"><a href="/wiki/File:MONROE,_James-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" class="image"><img alt="BEP engraved portrait of Monroe as President." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/MONROE%2C_James-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/300px-MONROE%2C_James-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg" width="300" height="367" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/MONROE%2C_James-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/450px-MONROE%2C_James-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/MONROE%2C_James-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/600px-MONROE%2C_James-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7912" data-file-height="9681" /></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MONROE,_James-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" title="File:MONROE, James-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg">File:MONROE, James-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg</a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Engraving_and_Printing" title="Bureau of Engraving and Printing">BEP</a> engraved portrait of Monroe as President.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_politics">Domestic politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Domestic politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Democratic-Republican_Party_dominance">Democratic-Republican Party dominance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Democratic-Republican Party dominance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Monroe largely ignored old party lines in making appointments to lower posts, which reduced political tensions and enabled the "<a href="/wiki/Era_of_Good_Feelings" title="Era of Good Feelings">Era of Good Feelings</a>", which lasted through his administration. He made two long national tours in 1817 to build national trust. Frequent stops on these tours allowed innumerable ceremonies of welcome and expressions of good will. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Federalist_Party" title="United States Federalist Party" class="mw-redirect">Federalist Party</a> continued to fade away during his administration; it maintained its vitality and organizational integrity in Delaware and a few localities, but lacked influence in national politics. Lacking serious opposition, the <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican Party</a>'s Congressional caucus stopped meeting, and for practical purposes the Democratic-Republican Party stopped operating.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_troubles">Domestic troubles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Domestic troubles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Monroe's popularity remained undiminished even when he followed difficult nationalist policies at a time when the country's commitment to nationalism started to show serious fractures.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1819" title="Panic of 1819">Panic of 1819</a> caused a painful economic depression. The application for statehood in 1819 by the <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Territory" title="Missouri Territory">Missouri Territory</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Slave_state" title="Slave state" class="mw-redirect">slave state</a> failed. An amended bill for gradually eliminating slavery in <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a> precipitated two years of bitter debate in Congress. The <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Compromise" title="Missouri Compromise">Missouri Compromise</a> bill of 1820 resolved the struggle, pairing Missouri as a slave state with <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a>, a free state, and barring slavery north of latitude 36/30' N forever. The Missouri Compromise lasted until 1854, when Stephen A. Douglas's Kansas-Nebraska Act repealed it. In 1857 Supreme Court Chief Justice Roger Taney held that it had always been unconstitutional, as part of the <i><a href="/wiki/Dred_Scott_v._Sandford" title="Dred Scott v. Sandford">Dred Scott</a></i> decision.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cumberland_Road">Cumberland Road</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Cumberland Road">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Congress demanded high subsidies for internal improvements, such as for the improvement of the <a href="/wiki/Cumberland_Road" title="Cumberland Road" class="mw-redirect">Cumberland Road</a>, during Monroe's presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> Monroe vetoed the <a href="/wiki/Cumberland_Road_Bill" title="Cumberland Road Bill" class="mw-redirect">Cumberland Road Bill</a>, which provided for yearly improvements to the road, because he believed it to be unconstitutional for the government to have such a large hand in what was essentially a civics bill deserving of attention on a state by state basis. This defiance underlined Monroe's populist ideals and added credit to the local offices that he was so fond of visiting on his speech tours.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Indigenous_American_policies">Indigenous American policies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Indigenous American policies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Seminole_Wars" title="Seminole Wars">Seminole Wars</a></div>
<p>Monroe sparked a constitutional controversy when, in 1817, he sent General <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a> to move against Spanish Florida to pursue hostile <a href="/wiki/Seminole_Indians" title="Seminole Indians" class="mw-redirect">Seminole Indians</a> and punish the Spanish for aiding them. News of Jackson's exploits ignited a congressional investigation of the 1st Seminole War. Dominated by Democratic-Republicans, the 15th Congress was generally expansionist and more likely to support the popular Jackson. Ulterior political agendas of many congressmen dismantled partisan and sectional coalitions, so that Jackson's opponents argued weakly and became easily discredited. After much debate, the House of Representatives voted down all resolutions that condemned Jackson in any way, thus implicitly endorsing Monroe's actions and leaving the issue surrounding the role of the executive with respect to war powers unanswered.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Monroe believed that the Indians must progress from the hunting stage to become an agricultural people, noting in 1817, "A hunter or savage state requires a greater extent of territory to sustain it than is compatible with progress and just claims of civilised life."<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> His proposals to speed up the assimilation process were ignored by Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy">Foreign policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Foreign policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish_Florida">Spanish Florida</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Spanish Florida">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Relations with Spain over the purchase of <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Florida" title="Spanish Florida">Spanish Florida</a> proved to be troublesome, especially after <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a> invaded that territory on what he believed to be the president's authorization, which Monroe later denied giving. But largely through the skillful work of John Quincy Adams, a <a href="/wiki/Adams-Onis_treaty" title="Adams-Onis treaty" class="mw-redirect">treaty</a> was signed with Spain in 1819 that ceded Florida to the United States in return for the assumption of $5,000,000 (about $92,592,593 in 2014 dollars) in claims and the relinquishment of any claims to Texas.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> Florida was ceded to the U.S. in 1821.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Monroe_Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Monroe Doctrine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</a></div>
<p>After the Napoleonic wars (which ended in 1815), almost all of Spain's and Portugal's colonies in Latin America revolted and declared independence. Americans welcomed this development as a validation of the spirit of <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">Republicanism</a>. Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a> suggested delaying formal recognition until Florida was secured. The problem of imperial invasion was intensified by a Russian claim to the Pacific coast down to the fifty-first parallel and simultaneous European pressure to have all of Latin America returned to its colonial status.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Monroe informed Congress in March 1822 that permanent stable governments had been established in the <a href="/wiki/United_Provinces_of_the_R%C3%ADo_de_la_Plata" title="United Provinces of the Río de la Plata" class="mw-redirect">United Provinces of the Río de la Plata</a> (the core of present-day <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>), <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>. Adams, under Monroe's supervision, wrote the instructions for the ministers (ambassadors) to these new countries. They declared that the policy of the United States was to uphold republican institutions and to seek treaties of commerce on a most-favored-nation basis. The United States would support inter-American congresses dedicated to the development of economic and political institutions fundamentally differing from those prevailing in Europe. The articulation of an "American system" distinct from that of Europe was a basic tenet of Monroe's policy toward Latin America. Monroe took pride as the United States was the first nation to extend recognition and to set an example to the rest of the world for its support of the "cause of liberty and humanity".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Monroe formally announced in his message to Congress on December 2, 1823, what was later called the <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</a>. He proclaimed that the Americas should be free from future European colonization and free from European interference in sovereign countries' affairs. It further stated the United States' intention to stay neutral in European wars and wars between European powers and their colonies, but to consider new colonies or interference with independent countries in the Americas as hostile acts toward the United States.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Although it is Monroe's most famous contribution to history, the speech was written by Adams, who designed the doctrine in cooperation with Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-Ammon.2C_James_Monroe.2C_pp_476.E2.80.9392_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ammon.2C_James_Monroe.2C_pp_476.E2.80.9392-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> Monroe and Adams realized that American recognition would not protect the new countries against military intervention to restore Spain's power. In October 1823, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Rush" title="Richard Rush">Richard Rush</a>, the American minister in London, advised that Foreign Secretary <a href="/wiki/George_Canning" title="George Canning">George Canning</a> was proposing that the U.S. and Britain jointly declare their opposition to European intervention. Britain, with its powerful navy, also opposed re-conquest of <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a> and suggested that the United States join in proclaiming a "hands off" policy. Galvanized by the British initiative, Monroe consulted with American leaders and then formulated a plan with Adams. Ex-Presidents Jefferson and Madison counseled Monroe to accept the offer, but Adams advised, "It would be more candid ... to avow our principles explicitly to Russia and France, than to come in as a cock-boat in the wake of the British man-of-war." Monroe accepted Adams' advice. Not only must Latin America be left alone, he warned, but also Russia must not encroach southward on the Pacific coast. "...the American continents," he stated, "by the free and independent condition which they have assumed and maintain, are henceforth not to be considered as subjects for future colonization by any European Power."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The Monroe Doctrine at the time of its adoption thus pertained more to the Russians in North America than to the former Spanish colonies. The result was a system of American isolationism under the sponsorship of the British navy. The Monroe Doctrine held that the United States considered the Western Hemisphere as no longer a place for European colonization; that any future effort to gain further political control in the hemisphere or to violate the independence of existing states would be treated as an act of hostility; and finally that there existed two different and incompatible political systems in the world. The United States, therefore, promised to refrain from intervention in European affairs and demanded Europe to abstain from interfering with American matters. There were few serious European attempts at intervention.<sup id="cite_ref-Ammon.2C_James_Monroe.2C_pp_476.E2.80.9392_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ammon.2C_James_Monroe.2C_pp_476.E2.80.9392-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration_and_Cabinet">Administration and Cabinet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Administration and Cabinet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Monroe made balanced Cabinet choices, naming a southerner, <a href="/wiki/John_C._Calhoun" title="John C. Calhoun">John C. Calhoun</a>, as Secretary of War, and a northerner, <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a>, as Secretary of State. Both proved outstanding, as Adams was a master diplomat<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> and Calhoun completely reorganized the War Department to overcome the serious deficiencies that had hobbled it during the War of 1812.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> Monroe decided on political grounds not to offer <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a> the State Department, and Clay turned down the War Department and remained Speaker of the House, so Monroe lacked an outstanding westerner in his cabinet.</p>
