<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judicial_appointments">Judicial appointments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Judicial appointments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_federal_judges_appointed_by_James_Monroe" title="List of federal judges appointed by James Monroe">List of federal judges appointed by James Monroe</a></div>
<p>Monroe appointed one Justice to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smith_Thompson" title="Smith Thompson">Smith Thompson</a>. He appointed 21 other federal judges, all to <a href="/wiki/United_States_district_court" title="United States district court">United States district courts</a>, as no vacancies occurred on the one circuit court existing at the time.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="States_admitted_to_the_Union">States admitted to the Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: States admitted to the Union">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>&#160;– December 10, 1817</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>&#160;– December 3, 1818</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>&#160;– December 14, 1819</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a>&#160;– March 15, 1820</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a>&#160;– August 10, 1821</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-presidency">Post-presidency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Post-presidency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Monroe_marker_at_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4248.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/James_Monroe_marker_at_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4248.JPG/170px-James_Monroe_marker_at_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4248.JPG" width="170" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/James_Monroe_marker_at_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4248.JPG/255px-James_Monroe_marker_at_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4248.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/James_Monroe_marker_at_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4248.JPG/340px-James_Monroe_marker_at_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4248.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4320" data-file-height="3240" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Monroe_marker_at_Univ._of_VA_IMG_4248.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Monroe once owned a farm at the location of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia" title="University of Virginia">University of Virginia</a> in <a href="/wiki/Charlottesville,_Virginia" title="Charlottesville, Virginia">Charlottesville</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When his presidency ended on March 4, 1825, James Monroe resided at <a href="/wiki/Brown_College_at_Monroe_Hill" title="Brown College at Monroe Hill">Monroe Hill</a>, what is now included in the grounds of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia" title="University of Virginia">University of Virginia</a>. He had operated the family farm from 1788 to 1817, but sold it in the first year of his presidency to the new college. He served on the college's Board of Visitors under Jefferson and under the second rector <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a>, both former presidents, almost until his death.</p>
<p>Monroe had racked up many debts during his years of public life. He sold off his Highland Plantation (now called <a href="/wiki/Ash_Lawn-Highland" title="Ash Lawn-Highland" class="mw-redirect">Ash Lawn-Highland</a>). It is now owned by his <i>alma mater</i>, the <a href="/wiki/College_of_William_and_Mary" title="College of William and Mary" class="mw-redirect">College of William and Mary</a>, which has opened it to the public as an historic site. Throughout his life, he was not financially solvent, and his wife's poor health made matters worse.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>He and his wife lived in <a href="/wiki/Oak_Hill_(James_Monroe_House)" title="Oak Hill (James Monroe House)">Oak Hill</a>, Virginia, until Elizabeth's death on September 23, 1830. In August 1825, the Monroes had received the <a href="/wiki/Marquis_de_Lafayette" title="Marquis de Lafayette" class="mw-redirect">Marquis de Lafayette</a> and President <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a> as guests there.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Monroe_Grave.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/James_Monroe_Grave.JPG/250px-James_Monroe_Grave.JPG" width="250" height="409" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/James_Monroe_Grave.JPG/375px-James_Monroe_Grave.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/James_Monroe_Grave.JPG/500px-James_Monroe_Grave.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2205" data-file-height="3610" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:James_Monroe_Grave.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Monroe's grave at Hollywood Cemetery. <a href="/wiki/John_Tyler" title="John Tyler">John Tyler</a>'s grave is visible in the background.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Upon Elizabeth's death in 1830, Monroe moved to <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> to live with his daughter Maria Hester Monroe Gouverneur who had married <a href="/wiki/Samuel_L._Gouverneur" title="Samuel L. Gouverneur">Samuel L. Gouverneur</a> in the White House. Monroe's health began to slowly fail by the end of the 1820s and John Quincy Adams visited him there in April 1831.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> Adams found him alert and eager to discuss the situation in Europe, but in ill health. Adams cut the visit short when he thought he was tiring Monroe.</p>
<p>Monroe died there from <a href="/wiki/Heart_failure" title="Heart failure">heart failure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a> on July 4, 1831, thus becoming the third president to have died on <a href="/wiki/Independence_Day_(United_States)" title="Independence Day (United States)">Independence Day</a>, July 4. His death came 55 years after the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Declaration_of_Independence" title="U.S. Declaration of Independence" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Declaration of Independence</a> was proclaimed and 5 years after the death of two other Founding Fathers who became Presidents: <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>. Monroe was originally buried in New York at the Gouverneur family's vault in the <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Marble_Cemetery" title="New York City Marble Cemetery">New York City Marble Cemetery</a>. Twenty-seven years later, in 1858, the body was re-interred to the President's Circle at the <a href="/wiki/Hollywood_Cemetery_(Richmond,_Virginia)" title="Hollywood Cemetery (Richmond, Virginia)">Hollywood Cemetery</a> in <a href="/wiki/Richmond,_Virginia" title="Richmond, Virginia">Richmond, Virginia</a>. The <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe_Tomb" title="James Monroe Tomb">James Monroe Tomb</a> is a U.S. <a href="/wiki/National_Historic_Landmark" title="National Historic Landmark">National Historic Landmark</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_beliefs">Religious beliefs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Religious beliefs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>"When it comes to Monroe's thoughts on religion," Bliss Isely notes, "less is known than that of any other President." No letters survive in which he discussed his religious beliefs. Nor did his friends, family or associates comment on his beliefs. Letters that do survive, such as ones written after the death of his son, contain no discussion of religion.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Monroe was raised in a family that belonged to the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> when it was the state church in Virginia before the Revolution. As an adult, he frequently attended <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_Church_in_the_United_States_of_America" title="Episcopal Church in the United States of America" class="mw-redirect">Episcopal</a> churches, though there is no record he ever took communion.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Some historians see "deistic tendencies" in his few references to an impersonal God.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> Unlike Jefferson, Monroe was rarely attacked as an atheist or infidel. In 1832 <a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Renwick_Willson&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="James Renwick Willson (page does not exist)">James Renwick Willson</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Reformed_Presbyterian_Church_of_North_America" title="Reformed Presbyterian Church of North America">Reformed Presbyterian</a> minister in Albany, New York, criticized Monroe for having "lived and died like a second-rate Athenian philosopher."<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As Secretary of State, Monroe dismissed <a href="/wiki/Mordecai_Manuel_Noah" title="Mordecai Manuel Noah">Mordecai Manuel Noah</a> in 1815 from his post as consul to <a href="/wiki/Tunis" title="Tunis">Tunis</a> because he was Jewish.<sup id="cite_ref-isbn0-7425-0181-7_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-7425-0181-7-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> Noah protested and gained letters from Adams, Jefferson, and Madison supporting church-state separation and tolerance for Jews.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Monroe may have believed in an interactive God for he said:</p>
<p>"If we persevere...we can not fail, under the favor of a gracious Providence...My fervent <a href="/wiki/Prayers" title="Prayers" class="mw-redirect">prayers</a> to the Almighty that He will be graciously pleased to continue to us that protection which He has already so conspicuously displayed in our favor."<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Slavery">Slavery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Slavery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Monroe owned dozens of <a href="/wiki/Slaves" title="Slaves" class="mw-redirect">slaves</a>. According to William Seale, he took several slaves with him to Washington to serve at the White House from 1817 to 1825. This was typical of other slaveholders, as Congress did not provide for domestic staff of the presidents at that time.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On October 15, 1799, as some <a href="/wiki/Slave_traders" title="Slave traders" class="mw-redirect">slave traders</a> tried to transport a group of slaves from Southampton to Georgia, the <a href="/wiki/Slave_revolt" title="Slave revolt" class="mw-redirect">slaves revolted</a> and killed the traders.<sup id="cite_ref-aptheker_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aptheker-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> According to Scheer's article on the subject, a nearby slave patrol responded and killed ten slaves on the spot in extrajudicial killings without the benefit of trial. Of the initial group, the patrol took five slaves alive. They were tried in an oyer and terminer court without the benefit of a jury,<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> and four were convicted. (The fifth pleaded <a href="/wiki/Benefit_of_clergy" title="Benefit of clergy">benefit of clergy</a> and was flogged and branded). Governor Monroe postponed the slaves' executions to check their identities; he granted a pardon to one, and allowed two to hang. The fourth died in jail from exposure to the cold. Scheer says that Monroe "help[ed] secure a modicum of civil protection for slaves sentenced to death for capital crimes."<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>When Monroe was Governor of Virginia in 1800, hundreds of slaves from Virginia planned to kidnap him, take <a href="/wiki/Richmond,_Virginia" title="Richmond, Virginia">Richmond</a>, and negotiate for their freedom. Due to a storm on August 30, they were unable to attack. What became known as <a href="/wiki/Gabriel_Prosser" title="Gabriel Prosser">Gabriel's slave conspiracy</a> became public knowledge.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In response, Governor Monroe called out the militia; the slave patrols soon captured some slaves accused of involvement. Sidbury says some trials had a few measures to prevent abuses, such as an appointed attorney, but they were "hardly 'fair'". <a href="/wiki/Slave_codes" title="Slave codes">Slave codes</a> prevented slaves from being treated like whites, and they were given quick trials without a jury.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> Monroe influenced the Executive Council to pardon and sell some slaves instead of hanging them.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> Historians say the Virginia courts executed between 26 and 35 slaves. None of the executed slaves had killed any whites because the uprising had been foiled before it began.<sup id="cite_ref-aptheker_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aptheker-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As president of Virginia's constitutional convention in the fall of 1829, Monroe reiterated his belief that slavery was a blight which, even as a British colony, Virginia had attempted to eradicate. "What was the origin of our slave population?" he rhetorically asked. "The evil commenced when we were in our Colonial state, but acts were passed by our Colonial Legislature, prohibiting the importation, of more slaves, into the Colony. These were rejected by the Crown." To the dismay of states' rights proponents, he was willing to accept the federal government's financial assistance to emancipate and transport freed slaves to other countries. At the convention, Monroe made his final public statement on slavery, proposing that Virginia <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_States" title="Abolitionism in the United States">emancipate</a> and deport its bondsmen with "the aid of the Union."<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Monroe was part of the <a href="/wiki/American_Colonization_Society" title="American Colonization Society">American Colonization Society</a> formed in 1816, the members of which included <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay" title="Henry Clay">Henry Clay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>. They found common ground with some abolitionists in supporting colonization. They helped send several thousand freed slaves to the new colony of <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a> in Africa from 1820 to 1840. Slave owners like Monroe and Jackson wanted to prevent free blacks from encouraging slaves in the South to rebel. With about $100,000 in Federal grant money, the organization also bought land for the freedmen in what is today Liberia.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> The capital of <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a> was named <a href="/wiki/Monrovia" title="Monrovia">Monrovia</a> after President Monroe.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Honors_and_memberships">Honors and memberships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Honors and memberships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Monroe was elected a member of the <a href="/wiki/American_Antiquarian_Society" title="American Antiquarian Society">American Antiquarian Society</a> in April 1818.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy_and_memory">Legacy and memory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Legacy and memory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-style ambox-Prose" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="48" data-file-height="48" /></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>is in a list format that may be better presented using <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Prose" title="Wikipedia:Prose" class="mw-redirect">prose</a>.</b> <span class="hide-when-compact">You can help by converting this section to prose, if <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Embedded_list" title="Wikipedia:Embedded list" class="mw-redirect">appropriate</a>. <a href="/wiki/Help:Editing" title="Help:Editing">Editing help</a> is available.</span> <small><i>(January 2014)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<ul>
<li>Since its 1824 renaming in his honor, the capital city of the West African country of <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a> has been named <a href="/wiki/Monrovia" title="Monrovia">Monrovia</a>. It is the only non-American capital city named after a U.S. President.</li>
<li>On December 12, 1954, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postal_Service" title="United States Postal Service">United States Postal Service</a> released a 5¢ <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Issue" title="Liberty Issue">Liberty Issue</a> <a href="/wiki/Postage_stamp" title="Postage stamp">postage stamp</a> honoring Monroe.</li>
<li>There are academic buildings named after him at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Mary_Washington" title="University of Mary Washington">University of Mary Washington</a>, <a href="/wiki/College_of_William_and_Mary" title="College of William and Mary" class="mw-redirect">College of William and Mary</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Mason_University" title="George Mason University">George Mason University</a>, and <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_University" title="George Washington University">George Washington University</a>. In addition, a statue of Monroe was dedicated in front of Tucker Hall on the campus of William &amp; Mary in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>The City of <a href="/wiki/Monroe,_Michigan" title="Monroe, Michigan">Monroe, Michigan</a> is also named for him.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Monroe_County,_Kentucky" title="Monroe County, Kentucky">Monroe County, Kentucky</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> established in 1820, was named for him, as was <a href="/wiki/Monroe_County,_New_York" title="Monroe County, New York">Monroe County, New York</a>, (1821) and <a href="/wiki/Monroe_County,_Pennsylvania" title="Monroe County, Pennsylvania">Monroe County, Pennsylvania</a> (1836) <a href="/wiki/Monroe_County,_West_Virginia" title="Monroe County, West Virginia">Monroe County, West Virginia</a> (1799) <sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Monroe_County,_Indiana" title="Monroe County, Indiana">Monroe County, Indiana</a></li>
<li>The City of <a href="/wiki/Monroe,_Georgia" title="Monroe, Georgia">Monroe, Georgia</a>, incorporated in 1821, is named for him.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Township,_Middlesex_County,_New_Jersey" title="Monroe Township, Middlesex County, New Jersey">Township of Monroe</a>, in central New Jersey, founded in 1838, bears his name as well.</li>
<li>Monroe was the last U.S. President to wear a <a href="/wiki/Wig" title="Wig">powdered wig tied in a queue</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Tricorne" title="Tricorne">tricorne hat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Culottes" title="Culottes">knee-breeches</a> according to the <a href="/wiki/1775%E2%80%931795_in_fashion#Men.27s_fashion" title="1775–1795 in fashion" class="mw-redirect">old-fashioned style of the 18th century</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> That earned him the nickname "The Last Cocked Hat".<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Monroe is the last president neither to have been photographed nor to have had a photographed predecessor: his portraits are preserved today only on paintings.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Monroe was the third consecutive President elected to two consecutive terms, which would not occur again until <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2012" title="United States presidential election, 2012">2012</a>.</li>
</ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bibliography">Bibliography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=James_Monroe&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Bibliography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li>Ammon, Harry. <i>James Monroe: The Quest for National Identity.</i> (1971, 2nd ed. 1990). 706 pp. standard scholarly biography <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.amazon.com/dp/0813912660">excerpt and text search</a></li>
<li>Ammon, Harry. "James Monroe" in Henry F. Graff ed., <i>The Presidents: A Reference History</i> (1997)</li>
<li>Bemis, Samuel Flagg. <i>John Quincy Adams and the Foundations of American Foreign Policy</i> (1949), the standard history of Monroe's foreign policy.</li>
<li>Cresson, William P. <i>James Monroe</i> (1946). 577 pp. good scholarly biography</li>
<li>Cunningham, Noble E., Jr. <i>The Presidency of James Monroe.</i> 1996. 246 pp. standard scholarly survey</li>
<li>Dangerfield, George. <i>Era of Good Feelings</i> (1953) <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.amazon.com/dp/159740425X">excerpt and text search</a></li>
<li>Dangerfield, George. <i>The Awakening of American Nationalism: 1815–1828</i> (1965) standard scholarly survey <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.amazon.com/dp/0881338230">excerpt and text search</a></li>
<li>Elkins, Stanley M. and Eric McKitrick. <i>The Age of Federalism</i> (1995). most advanced analysis of the politics of the 1790s. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.questia.com/PM.qst?a=o&amp;se=gglsc&amp;d=5000304374">online edition</a></li>
<li>Heidler, David S. "The Politics of National Aggression: Congress and the First Seminole War," <i>Journal of the Early Republic</i> 1993 13(4): 501–530. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.jstor.org/stable/3124558">in JSTOR</a></li>
<li>Finkelman, Paul, ed. <i>Encyclopedia of the New American Nation, 1754–1829</i> (2005), 1600 pp.</li>
<li>Gilman, Daniel Coit. <i>James Monroe</i> (1911) 312 pages; old barely adequate biography. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/jamesmonroe01jamegoog">online edition</a></li>
<li>Hart, Gary. <i>James Monroe</i> (2005) superficial, short, popular biography</li>
<li>Howe, Daniel Walker. <i>What Hath God Wrought: The Transformation of America, 1815–1848</i> (2007), Pulitzer Prize; a sweeping interpretation of the entire era</li>
<li>Holmes, David L. <i>The Faiths of the Founding Fathers</i>, May 2006, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.vqronline.org/articles/2003/autumn/holmes-religion-james-monroe/">online version</a></li>
<li>Kranish, Michael. "At Capitol, slavery's story turns full circle", The Boston GLobe, Boston, December 28, 2008.</li>
<li>May, Ernest R. <i>The Making of the Monroe Doctrine</i> (1975), argues it was issued to influence the outcome of the presidential election of 1824.</li>
<li>Morgan, George. <i>The Life of James Monroe</i> (1921) 484 pages; old and barely adequate biography. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/lifejamesmonroe00morggoog">online edition</a></li>
<li>Perkins, Bradford. <i>Castlereagh and Adams: England and the United States, 1812–1823</i> (1964)</li>
<li>Perkins, Dexter. <i>The Monroe Doctrine, 1823–1826</i> (1927), the standard monograph about the origins of the doctrine.</li>
<li><span class="languageicon" style="font-size:0.95em; font-weight:bold; color:#555;">(Italian)</span> <a href="/wiki/Nico_Perrone" title="Nico Perrone">Nico Perrone</a>, <i>Progetto di un impero. 1823. L'annuncio dell'egemonia americana infiamma la borsa</i> (Project of an Empire. 1823. The Announcement of American Hegemony Inflames the Stock Exchange), Naples, La Città del Sole, 2013 <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9788882923105" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 978-88-8292-310-5</a></li>
<li>Powell, Walter &amp; Steinberg, Richard. <i>The nonprofit sector: a research handbook</i>, Yale, 2006, p. 40.</li>
<li>Renehan Edward J., Jr. <i>The Monroe Doctrine: The Cornerstone of American Foreign Policy</i> (2007)</li>
<li>Scherr, Arthur. "James Monroe and John Adams: An Unlikely 'Friendship'". <i>The Historian</i> 67#3 (2005) pp 405+. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.questia.com/read/5019026982">online edition</a></li>
<li>Skeen, Carl Edward. <i>1816: America Rising</i> (1993) popular history</li>
<li>Scherr, Arthur. "James Monroe on the Presidency and 'Foreign Influence;: from the Virginia Ratifying Convention (1788) to Jefferson's Election (1801)." <i>Mid-America</i> 2002 84(1–3): 145–206. ISSN 0026-2927.</li>
<li>Scherr, Arthur. "Governor James Monroe and the Southampton Slave Resistance of 1799." <i>Historian</i> 1999 61(3): 557–578. ISSN 0018-2370 Fulltext online in SwetsWise and Ebsco.</li>
<li>Styron, Arthur. <i>The Last of the Cocked Hats: James Monroe and the Virginia Dynasty</i> (1945). 480 pp. thorough, scholarly treatment of the man and his times.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Harlow_Unger" title="Harlow Unger">Unger, Harlow G</a>.. "<i><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://dacapopress.com/book/paperback/the-last-founding-father/9780306819186">The Last Founding Father: James Monroe and a Nation's Call to Greatness</a></i>" (2009), a new biography.</li>
<li>White, Leonard D. <i>The Jeffersonians: A Study in Administrative History, 1801–1829</i> (1951), explains the operation and organization of federal administration</li>
<li>Whitaker, Arthur P. <i>The United States and the Independence of Latin America</i> (1941)</li>
<li>Wilmerding, Jr., Lucius, <i>James Monroe: Public Claimant</i> (1960) A study regarding Monroe's attempts to get reimbursement for personal expenses and losses from his years in public service after his Presidency ended.</li>
<li>Wood, Gordon S. <i>Empire of Liberty: A history of the Early Republic, 1789–1815</i> (2009)</li>
</ul>
